   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d106950139" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Hard carbon (HC) has been widely regarded as the most promising anode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) due to its decent capacity and low cost. However, the poor initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of HC seriously hinders its practical application in SIBs. Herein, pyridinic N-doped hard carbon polyhedra with easily accessible carbonyl groups and in situ coupled carbon nanotubes are rationally synthesized via a facile pretreated zeolitic imidazolate framework (ZIFs)-carbonization strategy. The comprehensive ex/in situ techniques combined with theoretical calculations reveal that the synergy of pyridinic-N and carbonyl groups promoted by the pretreatment and carbonization process would not only optimize the Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption energy but also accelerate the desorption of Na<sup>+</sup>, significantly suppressing the irreversible capacity loss. As a result, the as-synthesized hard carbon polyhedra as an anode can deliver an unprecedented high ICE of 98% with a large reversible capacity of 389.4 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup>. This work may provide an effective strategy for the structural design of HC with high ICE.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403144-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are emerging as one of the most promising alternatives to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), owing to the abundance of sodium sources and their similar working principle to LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0001_R_d106950127e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Adv. Mater.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0002_R_d106950127e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, graphite as a commercialized anode material for LIBs suffers from limited sodium storage capacity because of the large ionic radius of sodium (1.02 Å for Na vs 0.76 Å for Li) and the instability of sodium intercalation compounds. Hard carbon (HC), with enlarged interlayer spacing, structural stability, and low operation potential, has been recognized as a feasible anode material for SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0003_R_d106950127e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Small Struct">3</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0004_R_d106950127e461" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Mater. Adv.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Generally, the sodium storage in HC can be characterized into two regions: i) a slope capacity in the high potential region above 0.1&nbsp;V, attributing to the adsorption of sodium ions on the intrinsic/extrinsic defects and the surface; ii) a plateau capacity at the low potential region below 0.1&nbsp;V, ascribing to the intercalation of sodium ions in the graphitic-like carbon layers or the filling of closed micropores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0005_R_d106950127e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a>-<a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0008_R_d106950127e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Based on the above perception, various methodologies have been implemented to refine the microstructure and electronic distribution of HC anodes, with the aim of enhancing their sodium storage capacity. Given that HC materials are frequently produced by calcining the precursor at high carbonization temperatures, their structural modulation can be achieved by tuning the carbonization process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0009_R_d106950127e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">9</a>-<a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0011_R_d106950127e474" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Small">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Several studies suggest that increasing the pyrolysis temperature and decreasing the heating rate can obtain HC with more ordered structures and fewer defects, leading to higher plateau capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0012_R_d106950127e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Mater. Chem. Front.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0013_R_d106950127e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Energy Environ. Sci.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, the introduction of metallic elements (e.g., Fe, Ca, and Mg) is found to be capable of catalyzing the formation of the graphite domain, which is beneficial to the bulk Na<sup>+</sup> transportation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0014_R_d106950127e487" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - RSC Adv.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Chou et&nbsp;al. demonstrated that Mn<sup>2+</sup> with a certain concentration could catalyze the construction of long-range graphene layers for Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and pore-filling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0015_R_d106950127e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Recently, they reported that a corncob-derived HC synthesized at 2000&nbsp;°C exhibited a full-plateau property, with a high plateau capacity of 290&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0016_R_d106950127e504" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the complex and highly graphitized structure may block the diffusion of Na<sup>+</sup> and limit the long-term cycling and rate performance.</p>
         
         <p>Heteroatom doping (e.g., B, N, O, S, and P) is a feasible approach to modulate the electronic density by disrupting the π-π conjugation system and forming electronegative functional groups that enhance Na<sup>+</sup> affinity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0017_R_d106950127e516" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Energy Sustainability Res.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As a result, it can not only improve the conductivity of HCs but also enhance the sloping capacity by introducing more active sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0018_R_d106950127e521" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhi et&nbsp;al. conducted theoretical calculations to guide the fabrication of N, S-co-doped HC sheets with a decent concentration of vacancy defects and porosity, achieving a high reversible capacity (430 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) and a good rate capability (277 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0019_R_d106950127e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Storage Mater.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Zhao et&nbsp;al. reported a series of N-doped carbon nanosheets derived from carboxymethylcellulose sodium (CMC-Na) and found that the optimized products exhibited a high reversible capacity of 304.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0020_R_d106950127e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the strong adsorption caused by heteroatom doping alone may increase the likelihood of so-called “trap effect”, which results in a diminished initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) and jeopardize commercial applications due to the limited sodium in the cathode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0021_R_d106950127e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0022_R_d106950127e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Mater. Chem. A">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, it is urgent to strike a balance between the capacity and ICE in doping engineering.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0023_R_d106950127e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Energy Storage Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0024_R_d106950127e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Herein, we reported a synthesis of a N-doped HC polyhedra with easily accessible carbonyl groups and in situ coupled carbon nanotubes (CNTs) (p-CNTs@HC) via a facile pretreated zeolitic imidazolate framework (p-ZIFs)-carbonization strategy. The pretreatment is not only favorable to the release of NO during the following pyrolysis but also induces more pyridinic-N and carbonyl groups within HC, leading to a larger d-spacing for Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation and the synergistically optimized adsorption/desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> to break the ICE/capacity trade-off. Accordingly, the as-developed p-CNTs@HC exhibits an excellent reversible capacity of 389.43 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup>, accompanied by an impressive ICE of 98%, representing a new record for the reported HCs to date.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403144-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The synthetic route for pyridinic N-dominated hard carbon with accessible C═O and coupled CNTs is schematically illustrated in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0001">1a</a>. First, core@shell ZIF-8@ZIF-67 precursor was pretreated at 200&nbsp;°C in the air to remove the residual organics and introduce more structural defects. After that, the pretreated precursor was carbonized at high temperatures under flowing nitrogen gas, during which Zn evaporated from ZIF-8 core and Co ions were reduced to metallic Co nanoparticles by the reducing gases generated from the pyrolysis. Meanwhile, with the assistance of the catalytic effect of Co nanoparticles, the coordinated organic ligands were converted to hard carbon with in situ decorated CNTs (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0025_R_d106950127e589" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Appl. Catal. B-Environ.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The final products are denoted as p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> (<i>x</i> = 900, 950, 1000, and 1050&nbsp;°C). For comparison, untreated ZIF-8@ZIF-67 precursor was also used to prepare CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> under the same carbonization condition. High-angle annular dark-field scanning transmission electron microscopy (HAADF-STEM) and elemental line scan results reveal that the original ZIF-8@ZIF-67 has a core-shell rhombic dodecahedron structure with Co-ZIF (ZIF-67) epitaxially growing on Zn-ZIF (ZIF-8) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0001">1b</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). After being pretreated at 200&nbsp;°C, the dodecahedral morphology is well-preserved with an almost unchanged particle size (Figure 1c; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Nevertheless, the Organic Elemental Microanalysis (OEM) result discloses a decrease in carbon, hydrogen, and oxygen contents, while the nitrogen content remains almost unchanged (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0001">1d</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0026_R_d106950127e617" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The change in element content may be attributed to the removal of residual solvents (i.e., methanol and H<sub>2</sub>O) on the surface of the precursor and the disruption of the coordination structure with introduced defects, which can be verified by a slightly broadened and diminished peaks between 25° and 40° in the X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403144-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/44af4f7c-d551-4d62-bf8c-21d4825e5b01/adfm202403144-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/44af4f7c-d551-4d62-bf8c-21d4825e5b01/adfm202403144-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aa2e9d20-4a35-40b1-8d39-49715643207a/adfm202403144-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/44af4f7c-d551-4d62-bf8c-21d4825e5b01/adfm202403144-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403144-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403144" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthesis of p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i>. The line scans and HAADF-STEM images of b) ZIF-8@ZIF-67 and c) p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67, respectively (Co: green; Zn: red; O: magenta; N: cyan). d) The OEM results for p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67 and ZIF-8@ZIF-67. e) TG spectra of p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67 and ZIF-8@ZIF-67 from the room temperature to 1000&nbsp;°C at the atmosphere of N<sub>2</sub>. f) The 3D figure and g) the contour map of the in situ FTIR spectra of carbonization products derived from p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Online thermogravimetry-Fourier transform infrared reflection spectrometry (TG-FTIR) was used to monitor the volatile gases released during the carbonization of p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67 and ZIF-8@ZIF-67. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0001">1e</a>, both precursors exhibit two mass loss stages and begin to decompose at ≈350&nbsp;°C, releasing small molecule gases such as C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>2</sub>, HCN, and N<sub>2</sub>/H<sub>3</sub>CN/CO.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0027_R_d106950127e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - J. Dispersion Sci. Technol.">27</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0028_R_d106950127e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Front. Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The maximum decomposition rate occurrs at 550&nbsp;°C due to the violation of NH<sub>3</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0001">1f,g</a>). However, p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67 exhibits a higher decomposition rate than ZIF-8@ZIF-67 as well as the promoted release of NO (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information), which may be related to the structure disruption caused by the pretreatment.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0018_R_d106950127e680" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0002">2a,b</a> presents transmission electron microscopy (TEM) images of the HC product obtained at 1000&nbsp;°C (denoted as p-CNTs@HCs-1000). It is observed that Co nanoparticles are encapsulated at the end of CNTs, in which CNTs were grafted onto the HC. The high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image displays lattice fringes of 0.203 and 0.355&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (111) facet of Co and the (002) facet of CNTs, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0002">2c</a>). Note that Co nanoparticles as well as CNTs can increase the conductivity of HC (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), which are difficult to be removed thoroughly (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0029_R_d106950127e702" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0002">2d–h</a> and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a> (Supporting Information) further show HRTEM images of the disordered structure of p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> and CNTs@HC-<i>x</i>. To measure the interlayer distance (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) of graphitic-like domains, we averaged three selected regions for precision in view of the disordered structure of HCs. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0002">2i</a>, the carbon skeleton evolves progressively with increasing pyrolysis temperature, resulting in an enlarged interlayer spacing that peaks at 1000&nbsp;°C. At 1050&nbsp;°C, the disordered domains are rearranged to generate more ordered graphitic layers, resulting in a reversal of the <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> value. This trend can be observed in both p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> and CNTs@HC-<i>x</i>. However, p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> generally exhibits larger interlayer distances than CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> synthesized at the same temperature. This may be attributed to the release of NO promoted by the pretreatment during the pyrolysis. Among them, p-CNTs@HCs-1000 demonstrates the largest <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> of 0.383&nbsp;nm.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403144-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/89813d97-d077-4b18-bfb6-69003b388e76/adfm202403144-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/89813d97-d077-4b18-bfb6-69003b388e76/adfm202403144-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a02fee12-c8fd-4624-b676-a6ad118a4d2b/adfm202403144-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/89813d97-d077-4b18-bfb6-69003b388e76/adfm202403144-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403144-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403144" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Low-and b) high-magnified TEM images of p-CNTs@HC-1000. c) HRTEM images of CNTs and Co nanoparticles in p-CNTs@HC-1000. The HRTEM images and the corresponding intensity line profiles of the HC regions for d) CNTs@HC-1000, e) p-CNTs@HC-900, f) p-CNTs@HC-950, g) p-CNTs@HC-1000 and h) p-CNTs@HC-1050 (scale bar is 5&nbsp;nm). i) Comparison of the interlayer distances (<i>d</i><sub>002</sub>) of the as-synthesized samples.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The disordered atomic structures of CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> and p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> were further characterized by XRD and Raman spectroscopy. As shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3a</a>, XRD patterns show broad peaks at ≈21° and relatively sharp peaks at 26.3°, which correspond to the (002) facets of HC and CNTs, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0008_R_d106950127e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, sharp peaks located at 44.2°, 51.5°, and 75.9° can be indexed to the (111), (200), and (220) crystal planes of the Co phase (PDF card 89–4307), respectively. Notably, due to the intense signals from Co nanoparticles, the signal of HC with low crystallinity is relatively weak. As a result, the (100) peak of HC at ≈43° is obscured in all samples, and the (002) peak of HC is absent in p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-950. Nevertheless, the (002) peak of HC shifts to lower degrees when the carbonization temperature increases from 950 to 1000&nbsp;°C, and returns to ≈21° at 1050&nbsp;°C, indicating the interlayer spacing of HCs is the largest at 1000&nbsp;°C, which is consistent with the HRTEM observations.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403144-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c4d06742-b983-4a1d-bdab-0ac41e0d53e4/adfm202403144-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c4d06742-b983-4a1d-bdab-0ac41e0d53e4/adfm202403144-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0432d041-76ef-4402-a3e7-cf44e0d021d5/adfm202403144-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c4d06742-b983-4a1d-bdab-0ac41e0d53e4/adfm202403144-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403144-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403144" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD patterns and b) Raman spectra of the p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> (abbreviate to “p-<i>x</i>”) and CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> (abbreviate to “<i>x</i>”). c) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption-desorption isotherms of p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-1000 (inset showing the pore size distribution). d) XPS survey spectra of p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-1000. High-resolution XPS spectra of e) N 1s, f) O 1s, and g) C 1s. h) N K-edge XANES spectra and i) O K-edge XANES spectra of p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-1000.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The Raman spectra of all samples present three characteristic peaks at 1340, 1569, and ≈2680 cm<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3b</a>), corresponding to the typical D-, G-, and 2D-band of carbon materials, respectively. The 2D-band is associated with the ordered graphene layer stacking within a short or medium range, confirming the presence of CNTs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0030_R_d106950127e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Prog. Mater. Sci.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The D-band is related to the breathing mode of the sp<sup>2</sup> C-bond, indicating structural defects, while the G-band represents the in-plane vibration.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0031_R_d106950127e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Diam. Relat. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> peak area ratio is a widely accepted indicator of carbon structural ordering. With increasing carbonization temperature, the G peak becomes narrower and sharper with decreased <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information), implying the increase of in-plane ordering. It is worth mentioning that p-CNTs@HC-1000 reaches an abnormally high <i>I</i><sub>D</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>G</sub> value of 1.16 on account of the introduction of defects by the pretreatment.</p>
         
         <p>The specific surface area (SSA) and the pore structure of the as-prepared samples were investigated using nitrogen adsorption-desorption measurements (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a>, Supporting Information). All samples display characteristic type-IV isotherms with an apparent hysteresis loop under high relative pressure, indicating the presence of mesopores and micropores.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0032_R_d106950127e865" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Int. J. Biol. Macromol.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The total pore volume, especially the mesopore volume, decreases with rising carbonization temperature, leading to a decrease in SSA (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information). Notably, the pretreatment is beneficial to the formation of micropores below 1&nbsp;nm while limits the generation of mesopores in p-CNTs@HC-1000, resulting in a lower SSA (204.3 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>) than that of CNTs@HC-1000 (216.3&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>). This unique pore structure as well as the appropriate SSA are expected to achieve a higher ICE by limiting the formation of SEI.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0033_R_d106950127e882" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To understand the effect of the pretreatment on the surface chemical environment of the HC, XPS spectra were collected from two representative samples synthesized at 1000&nbsp;°C. The overall survey spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3d</a> show four distinct signals of C 1s, O 1s, N 1s, and Co 2p. Notably, the nitrogen content decreases slightly from 2.88 at% for CNTs@HC-1000 to 2.35 at% for p-CNTs@HC-1000 (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). This can be ascribed to the increased amount of NO escaping during the carbonization process as a result of the pretreatment. The high-resolution N 1s spectra can be deconvoluted as pyridinic-N (399&nbsp;eV), pyrrolic-N (401&nbsp;eV), and graphitic-N (402&nbsp;eV) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0018_R_d106950127e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0020_R_d106950127e898" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, p-CNTs@HC-1000 shows a much higher content of pyridinic-N (41.4 at%) but a lower content of graphitic-N (38.4 at%) in comparison to CNTs@HC-1000 (25.6 at% and 55.2 at% for contents of pyridinic-N and graphitic-N, respectively) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting a formation of higher pyridinic-N after the carbonization of pretreated ZIF sample. The high-resolution O 1s spectra exhibited three peaks located at 531.5, 533.0, and 534.5&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3f</a>), which are assigned to C═O, C─O, and the adsorbed H<sub>2</sub>O, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0034_R_d106950127e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Commun.">34</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0035_R_d106950127e912" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Interestingly, the relative content of C═O increases, whereas that of C─O decreases in the carbonization of the pretreated ZIF sample. It is widely recognized that C═O serves as the active site for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, while C─O may have a detrimental impact on the ICE.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0033_R_d106950127e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">33</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0036_R_d106950127e920" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, the tailored O species resulting from the pretreatment is expected to benefit for the sodium storage performance of p-CNTs@HC-1000. The high-resolution C 1s spectra feature four peaks at 284.8, 285.2, 286.6, and 290.4&nbsp;eV, corresponding to C─C, C─N, C─O, and C═O, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3g</a>).</p>
         
         <p>The X-ray absorption near-edge spectroscopy (XANES) was implemented for further elucidation of the electronic structure of p-CNTs@HC-1000. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3h</a> shows the XANES spectra of N K-edge, in which five main features labeled N1 (≈398.0&nbsp;eV), N2 (399.0&nbsp;eV), N3 (399.7&nbsp;eV), N4 (401.2&nbsp;eV), and N5 (≈407.0&nbsp;eV) can be observed. Pyridinic-, pyrrolic-, and graphitic-N present at N1, N3, and N4, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0037_R_d106950127e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nano-Micro Lett.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0038_R_d106950127e934" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nat. Commun.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The contents of pyridinic-N and graphitic-N are higher for p-CNTs@HC-1000 than those for CNTs@HC-1000, respectively, which is in agreement with the XPS results. Additionally, N5 refers to the σ<sup>*</sup> excitation of C─N, which exhibits a lower content of N in p-CNTs@HC-1000 in comparison with that of CNTs@HC-1000. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3i</a> displays four distinct features in the O K-edge XANES spectra. O1 (530.7&nbsp;eV), O2 (532.2&nbsp;eV), and O3 (534.8&nbsp;eV) correspond to the π* excitation of C═O, C─O, and OH due to the adsorption of H<sub>2</sub>O, respectively. A broad peak located at ≈541.4&nbsp;eV is related to the σ* excitation of C═O/ C─O.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0039_R_d106950127e947" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Chem. Commun.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> There is a noticeable increase in C═O and a decrease in C─O in p-CNTs@HC-1000 compared to CNTs@HC-1000, which is in line with the trend observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0003">3f</a>. It is noteworthy that the Co L-edge XANES spectra of p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-1000 almost overlap (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting the similar electronic structure of Co for these two samples.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical performance of p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-1000 was first evaluated using cyclic voltammetry (CV). As shown by the first three cycles of CV curves of p-CNTs@HC-1000 tested at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0004">4a</a>, the redox pair located at ≈0.03/0.3&nbsp;V corresponds to the intercalation/deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into graphitic-like layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0040_R_d106950127e972" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In addition, another pair of redox peaks at 2.35 and 2.7&nbsp;V represents the reaction between the carbonyl groups and the sodium ions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0024_R_d106950127e978" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - J. Mater. Chem. A">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The wide potential window ranges from 1.0 to 2.0&nbsp;V, indicating a typical capacitive storage behavior resulting from the adsorption on defects and heteroatom-containing functional groups.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0041_R_d106950127e983" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A cathodic peak at ≈0.6&nbsp;V exists in the initial cycle and then disappears in the following cycle, implying the decomposition of the electrolyte to form a solid electrolyte (SEI) film.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0042_R_d106950127e989" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - ACS Nano">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Although CNTs@HC-1000 exhibits similar CV curves to p-CNTs@HC-1000 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information), the redox pair of sodium intercalation/deintercalation is broadened, suggesting a narrowed interlayer spacing.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403144-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dae80fcc-8461-4a41-8d81-f077966b6af7/adfm202403144-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dae80fcc-8461-4a41-8d81-f077966b6af7/adfm202403144-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d49adf7c-d1b2-4ac3-b99a-382ae9422387/adfm202403144-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dae80fcc-8461-4a41-8d81-f077966b6af7/adfm202403144-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403144-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403144" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of p-CNTs@HC-1000 at the scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) The first cycle of GCD curves of at the current density of 0.1C (30&nbsp;mA&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>) of p-CNTs@HC-1000, CNTs@HC-1000, and HC-1000. c) Comparison of specific capacity and ICE of samples reported in different works. d) Rate capability and e) long-term cycling performance (1 C) of p-CNTs@HC-1000, CNTs@HC-1000, and HC-1000. f) CV curves at different scan rates, g) plots of log (scan rate) versus log (peak current), and h) the charge contribution ratio of capacitance and diffusion process at different scan rates of p-CNTs@HC-1000.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0004">4b</a> presents the reversible charge-discharge profiles of p-CNTs@HC-1000, CNTs@HC-1000, and HC-1000. All the galvanostatic charge-discharge (GCD) curves exhibit large sloping regions with limited plateau regions, indicating an adsorption-dominated “adsorption-intercalation” mechanism. Among them, HC-1000 obtained via carbonization of ZIF-8 at 1000&nbsp;°C demonstrates the lowest reversible capacity at all current densities compared to the core-shell ZIF-derived anodes, possibly due to its poor sodium intercalation/deintercalation ability and low electrical conductivity. In contrast, p-CNTs@HC-1000 achieves an impressive ICE of 98% and a reversible capacity of 389.43&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, which not only exceeds those of CNTs@HC-1000 (77.1% and 205.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>) but also are superior to those of previously reported heteroatom-doped HCs or HC-based composites (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0004">4c</a>; Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0015_R_d106950127e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Energy Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0018_R_d106950127e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Small">18</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0020_R_d106950127e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Small">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0029_R_d106950127e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J.">29</a>, <a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0043_R_d106950127e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">43</a>-<a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0049_R_d106950127e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Such a remarkable performance can be ascribed to three main reasons. First, the abundance of active sites, especially pyridinic-N and C═O could effectively adsorb Na<sup>+</sup> during the discharge process above 0.1&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup>. Second, the expanded interlayer spacing benefits the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> below 0.1&nbsp;V, thereby achieving an increased plateau capacity of 71.8 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. Additionally, the reduced SSA and the moderate adsorption energy may contribute to the high ICE, which will be discussed later.</p>
         
         <p>The rate performance of p-CNTs@HC-1000, CNTs@HC-1000, and HC-1000 are compared in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0004">4d</a>. Obviously, the p-CNTs@HC-1000 exhibits an excellent rate performance with specific capacities of 389.4, 332.4, 259.8, 243.4, and 212.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, and 2 C, respectively, much higher than those of CNTs@HC-1000 and HC-1000 anodes. Even at high current density of 5&nbsp;C, the specific capacity for p-CNTs@HC-1000 still remains 170 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and can return to the pristine value when the current density is back to 0.1 C, confirming its remarkable rate performance. Note that both samples display a capacity increase during the initial test rate at 0.1 C, which aligns with the activation process observed in the CV test (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0004">4a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a>, Supporting Information). Furthermore, p-CNTs@HC-1000 maintains a capacity of 193.08 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 C after 200 cycles, while CNTs@HC-1000 can only achieve a capacity of 144.96 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under the same condition (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0004">4e</a>). The full cell performance was evaluated with the O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) cathode (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). The NFM//p-CNTs@HC-1000 with a N/P ratio of 1.05 displays higher discharging capacity than NFM//CNTs@HC-1000 with a N/P ratio of 1.25 (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>CV tests were further conducted at various scan rates to analyze the sodium storage behavior of p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-1000 (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0004">4f</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). According to the Power law, the current (<i>i</i>) at a given voltage and scan rate (<i>v</i>) are correlated exponentially as <i>i</i>  =  <i>av<sup>b</sup></i>, where <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> are constants.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0050_R_d106950127e1114" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The variation of the exponent <i>b</i> is regarded as an indicator of the dominant reaction mechanism. The value of <i>b</i> close to 0.5 and 1.0 corresponds to the diffusion-controlled and surface-controlled processes, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0035_R_d106950127e1124" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Energy Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the cathodic peaks at 0.01&nbsp;V, the <i>b</i> value of p-CNTs@HC-1000 is calculated to be 0.672, which is higher than that of CNTs@HC-1000 (0.655). The results suggest that the insertion of sodium ions into the graphitic-like domains of p-CNTs@HC-1000 is diffusion-controlled and facilitated by the increased interlayer spacing (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0002">2i</a>). Interestingly, the <i>b</i> value of p-CNTs@HC-1000 increases to 0.769, as measured by the anodic peak at 0.3–0.5&nbsp;V, while that of CNTs@HC-1000 remains below 0.75. This indicates that the dominant behavior during the charging process in p-CNTs@HC-1000 is converted to surface capacitance behavior, which can promote the desodiation and is beneficial to the ICE. By contrast, CNTs@HC-1000 remains a sluggish diffusion-controlled desodiation behavior, possibly due to the fact that Na<sup>+</sup> ions are trapped on defects.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0051_R_d106950127e1139" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The pseudo-capacity proportions of p-CNTs@HC-1000 are quantified in terms of <i>i</i>(<i>v</i>) = <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> + <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup>, and contribute 62.95%, 73.36%, 78.56%, 84.56%, and 98.27% to the total capacities at 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, which are also higher than those of CNTs@HC-1000. This facilitated sodium storage process may be ascribed to the modulation of the sodium adsorption energy as a result of the improved amount of pyridinic-N and C═O.</p>
         
         <p>Considering that the intensity and position of D- and G-band is greatly affected by the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the HC, the in situ Raman spectroscopy was employed to investigate the structural evolution of p-CNTs@HC-1000 during the discharging and charging process. The spectra were collected every 10&nbsp;min during the GCD test performed at 0.2&nbsp;C. As displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0005">5a</a>, during the initial discharge from the open circuit voltage (OCV) to 0.959&nbsp;V, both the D peak and G peak experience a red shift, suggesting the adsorption on the edge of graphitic-like domains. Subsequently, the D peak continues shifting to lower frequencies with gradually decreasing intensity, while the G peak remains constant until discharging to 0.139&nbsp;V. This indicates that Na<sup>+</sup> ions are predominantly stored on the defect and the surface during this process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0052_R_d106950127e1173" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Adv. Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the discharge process below 0.1&nbsp;V, the G peak is observed with a slight red-shift from 1566 to 1555 cm<sup>−1</sup> while D peak is unchanged, indicating the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into graphitic layers with the electron occupation in the π<sup>*</sup> band.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0053_R_d106950127e1185" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The desodiation process demonstrates three distinct stages (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0005">5b</a>). During the charging process below 0.942&nbsp;V, the D peak remains unchanged, while the G peak gradually shifts to a higher frequency. This phenomenon implies that the Na<sup>+</sup> extraction dominates this process, while Na<sup>+</sup> desorption is hindered by strong sodium-heteroatom interaction. However, the D peak exhibits an apparent blue-shift from 1273 to 1343 cm<sup>−1</sup> when charging from 0.972 to 1.723&nbsp;V, which is a rapid variation compared to the gradually shifted G peak. According to the CV tests conducted at different scan rates, the accelerated Na<sup>+</sup> desorption may be ascribed to the surface-controlled behavior. In the following charging process, the D peak remains unchanged, whereas the G peak gradually recovers to the pristine state, demonstrating the continuous extraction of intercalated Na<sup>+</sup>. Hence, the reversible capacity and ICE are significantly enhanced by the desorption-dominated desodiation from around 1.0 to 1.75&nbsp;V, meaning the successful inhibition of “trap effect”.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403144-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/b4801e23-7a9e-4b5b-9465-6a40d0c695e8/adfm202403144-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/b4801e23-7a9e-4b5b-9465-6a40d0c695e8/adfm202403144-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d9e449f0-802a-47fd-ae98-31f8cff429ac/adfm202403144-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/b4801e23-7a9e-4b5b-9465-6a40d0c695e8/adfm202403144-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403144-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403144" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The in situ Raman spectra of p-CNTs@HC-1000 during the a) discharging and b) charging process. c) In situ XRD patterns of p-CNTs@HC-1000 between 23° and 27°, and d) the 2D contour image from 40° to 80°.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In situ XRD analysis was further employed to investigate the sodium-storage behavior of p-CNTs@HC-1000 anode. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0005">5c</a> shows two series of peaks located at ≈24.3° and 25.3°, corresponding to the (002) plane of HC and CNTs, respectively. In particular, the (002) peak of HC undergoes a shift toward the lower degree with a minute extension during the discharge progress below 0.1&nbsp;V, and returns to its original degree upon charging to OCV. This phenomenon confirms the “adsorption-intercalation” mechanism in the discharging process and the gradual deintercalation during the whole charging process. In contrast, the (002) peak of CNTs remains unchanged during the discharging/charging process because its narrow interlayer spacing is unfavorable for the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup>. Similarly, the constant peaks assigned to Co crystals in the region from 40° to 80° can also be observed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0005">5d</a>. Therefore, CNTs and Co nanoparticles may only enhance the conductivity of HC rather than participate in sodium storage (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>In situ EIS analysis was conducted to monitor the generation of SEI on the surface of p-CNT@HC-1000 during the first discharge/charge process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0054_R_d106950127e1241" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - J. Phys. Chem. C">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information), a small semicircle with an almost unchanged diameter can be observed in the EIS curves, implying the initial <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> is rather low (≈2.2 Ω). After being charged to 2.5&nbsp;V, <i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub> increases to 19.54 Ω, indicating the formation of SEI film. It is noteworthy that another semicircle appears in both charge and discharge processes at the state of 0.6&nbsp;V. It may be attributed to the decomposition of electrolyte, resulting in the increase of the charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0055_R_d106950127e1259" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - Nano Res.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The element content of the SEI film formed on the anode was investigated by XPS depth profile analysis. As illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information), the Na content in both p-CNTs@HC-1000 and CNTs@HC-1000 anodes rises with increasing depth, indicating that the loss of Na occurs mainly in the early stage of SEI formation. However, the Na content in the SEI for the p-CNT@HCs-1000 is lower than that for CNTs@HC-1000 at the same depth. Meanwhile, the Na content demonstrates a greater reduction with the growth of SEI. This suppressed Na<sup>+</sup>-trapping during the SEI generation on the p-CNT@HCs-1000 anode is favorable to the high ICE, and may be attributed to the synergy of pyridinic-N and C═O.</p>
         
         <p>The high-resolution XPS spectra fitting peaks reveal the chemical composition of the SEI film (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18</a>, Supporting Information). Before etching (0&nbsp;nm), the p-CNTs@HC-1000 anode exhibits peaks at 284.8&nbsp;eV (C─C), 286.2&nbsp;eV (C─O), and 287.2&nbsp;eV (C═O) for C 1s spectra and the peak at 532.7&nbsp;eV for O 1s spectra, indicating the organic species originated from the decomposition of the solvent.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0056_R_d106950127e1276" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Adv. Mater.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The F 1s spectra display two distinct peaks at 684.1 and 689.9&nbsp;eV, corresponding to C─F and NaF, respectively. In comparison with that of CNTs@HC-1000, the proportion of NaF is larger in the outer SEI film for p-CNTs@HC-1000 and continues to increase with the etching depth. Given the ability of NaF to enhance the conductivity and morphology of the SEI,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0057_R_d106950127e1281" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - ACS Nano">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while C─F can prevent the dissolution of SEI and the continuous decomposition of electrolyte,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0058_R_d106950127e1287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Adv. Sci.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the SEI film on the p-CNTs@HC-1000 anode is presumed to be stable and conductive. For S 2p spectra, a doublet peak (166.5&nbsp;eV and 167.5&nbsp;eV) of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> and a peak assigned to Na<sub>2</sub>S can be observed along the SEI film for p-CNTs@HC-1000.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0059_R_d106950127e1299" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Nat. Commun.">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, another doublet peak of Na<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> dominates the S 2p spectra of CNTs@HC-1000. The difference of S species in the SEI may be due to the different surface chemical environments.</p>
         
         <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were conducted to unveil the impact of the N species on the sodium adsorption energy of HC. Considering the existence and advantage of carbonyl in HC, a model with a 5&nbsp;×&nbsp;5&nbsp;×&nbsp;1 supercell of graphene including a fixed oxygen atom attached to its edge was rationally built (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0006">6a–c</a>). Compared to the relaxed configuration of solely O-doped graphene (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a>, Supporting Information), the introduction of all species of N causes a greater distortion to the perfect graphene structure, leading to a higher degree of disorder and an expansion of <i>d</i><sub>002</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0006">6d–f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0026_R_d106950127e1327" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Inorg. Chem. Front.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The calculations on adsorption energy (<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>) of each N species for sodium ions indicate that pyrrolic-N has the lowest absolute value of <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> (−1.27&nbsp;eV), only slightly higher than that of the O-doped graphene (−0.77&nbsp;eV), which may be ascribed to the synergy of C═O and pyrrolic-N. On the contrary, the <i>E</i><sub>ads</sub> of the graphitic-N and pyridinic-N are calculated to be −1.65 and −2.15&nbsp;eV, respectively, representing more conductive to capture Na<sup>+</sup> due to the stronger cohesion energy. Note that high ICE requires both high adsorption energy and strong desorption ability to avoid irreversible capacity loss.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0060_R_d106950127e1344" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Adv. Energy Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For pyridinic-N (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0006">6a,d,g</a>), the nitrogen atom is opposite to the oxygen atom and both atoms are repelled to the sides of the graphene plane by the surrounding negative charge. In spite of the intense negative charge of O and pyridinic-N, positive charges are distributed around the carbon matrix, thus promoting the desorption of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. On the other hand, there are fewer positive charges distributed than negative charges for graphitic-N (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0006">6i</a>), which may prevent Na<sup>+</sup> ions from escaping and harming the ICE. Therefore, pyridinic-N is more beneficial to the reversible capacity of HC. Moreover, according to the relaxed configurations of all N species, Na<sup>+</sup> ions tend to adsorb on the O-site or the center of the conjugated carbon ring instead of the N-site. This is due to the higher electronic density of oxygen compared to nitrogen, revealing the doped nitrogen may function as a charge modulator. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202403144-fig-0006">6j</a> schematically shows the sodium storage of p-CNTs@HC-1000 anode. In the discharging process, the p-CNTs@HC-1000 anode follows the “adsorption-intercalation” mechanism (Stage I and II). In the charging process, the intercalated Na<sup>+</sup> ions continue to extract from the carbon layers but primarily dominates in stages III and V. Meanwhile, due to the charge distribution synergistically modulated by pyridinic-N and carbonyl, the desorption process can be effectively promoted between 1.0 and 1.75&nbsp;V versus Na/Na<sup>+</sup> (stage IV), resulting in an ideal reversible capacity.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adfm202403144-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/8cc737bb-3d3b-4dc1-9055-2c4c170f9fdc/adfm202403144-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/8cc737bb-3d3b-4dc1-9055-2c4c170f9fdc/adfm202403144-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/57bea00b-ba9f-4aef-ae7d-238710fdd3cd/adfm202403144-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/8cc737bb-3d3b-4dc1-9055-2c4c170f9fdc/adfm202403144-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202403144-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202403144" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">DFT calculations. The top and side-views of the sodium absorption on the O-doped graphene with a,d) the pyridinic-N, b,e) the pyrrolic-N, and c,f) the graphitic-N (brown: carbon; red: oxygen; silver: nitrogen; pink: hydrogen). Charge distribution plots of the O-doped graphene with g) the pyridinic-N, h) the pyrrolic-N, and i) the graphitic-N (cyan: negative charge; yellow: positive charge). j) The schematic illustration of the sodium storage mechanism in p-CNTs@HC-1000.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403144-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we have demonstrated a pretreated core@shell ZIF-driven strategy for the synthesis of a pyridinic N-doped hard carbon polyhedra with easily accessible carbonyl groups and in situ coupled carbon nanotubes for an efficient sodium storage. Ex situ/in situ characterizations combined with theoretical calculations reveal that the pretreatment of ZIF precursor is beneficial to the formation of pyridinic-N and C═O during the carbonization process, which not only optimizes the disordered structure for facile Na<sup>+</sup> ions intercalation/deintercalation but also provides abundant active sites for the enhanced adsorption/desorption ability of Na<sup>+</sup>. Furthermore, the synergistically modulated surface environment of the anode boosts a conductive and stable SEI film with adequate inorganic composition on the outer layer. The pretreated ZIF-derived hard carbon anode can achieve an unprecedented ICE of 98% with a high reversible capacity of 389.4&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.03 A g<sup>−1</sup> for sodium-ion batteries. This work may shed light on the effect of pretreated precursor on the modulation of carbon microstructures and pave a new avenue for the design of high-performance hard carbon anode for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202403144-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0070-title"> Synthesis of ZIF-8</h4>
                  
                  <p>ZIF-8 crystals were prepared by a coprecipitation method. First, 11.3&nbsp;g of 2-Melm was dissolved in 164&nbsp;mL of deionized water with the ultrasonic treatment to form a homogenous solution. Then, 725&nbsp;mg of Zn(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and 20&nbsp;mg of CTAB were added to the 2-Melm solution and stirred for an hour to form ZIF-8. The resulting suspension was centrifuged, washed with a mixture of methanol and deionized water for five times, and dried at 60&nbsp;°C overnight, to generate ZIF nanocrystals.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0080-title"> Synthesis of ZIF-8@ZIF-67</h4>
                  
                  <p>ZIF-8@ZIF-67 was prepared by the seed-mediated growth method, where ZIF-8 serves as the seed particle and ZIF-67 as the external crystal.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202403144-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202403144-bib-0061_R_d106950127e1438" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - ACS Nano">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Typically, 190&nbsp;mg of CoCl<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O and 3&nbsp;mg of CTAB were dissolved in a methanol solution. ZIF-8 nanoparticles were fully dispersed in the solution after 30&nbsp;min of sonication. Then, a 2-Melm (895&nbsp;mg) solution dissolved in 5&nbsp;mL of methanol was mixed with the above solution. After stirring for 10&nbsp;min, the solution was poured into a PTFE autoclave and maintained at 180&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The resulting purple precipitate was centrifuged, washed with a mixture of methanol and deionized water, and dried at 60&nbsp;°C overnight.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0090-title"> Preparation of p-CNTs@HC-x and CNTs@HC-x</h4>
                  
                  <p>The ZIF-8@ZIF-67 powder was pretreated by heating it at 200&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h in air with a heating rate of 2&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup>. The obtained p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67 was then heated at 900, 950, 1000, or 1050&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h under a nitrogen atmosphere at a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup>. The resulting samples were denoted as p-CNTs@HC-<i>x</i>, where <i>x</i> refers to the carbonization temperature. The synthesis of CNTs@HC-<i>x</i> was similar to that of p-CNTs@HC<i>-x</i>, except that the precursor is ZIF-8@ZIF-67 instead of p-ZIF-8@ZIF-67.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0100-title"> Preparation of HC-1000</h4>
                  
                  <p>ZIF-8 was heated at 1000&nbsp;°C for 2&nbsp;h under a nitrogen atmosphere at a heating rate of 5&nbsp;°C min<sup>−1</sup>. The resulting powder was named HC-1000.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0110-title"> Materials Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The morphology of the as-synthesized samples was characterized by scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and high-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM, JEOL, JEM-2100F). The phase structures were identified through X-ray diffraction (XRD, Bruker, D8 Advance) with Cu Kα radiation (<i>λ</i> = 0.15418&nbsp;nm) and Raman spectroscopy (Renishaw inVia Qontor) with a 532&nbsp;nm laser excitation. N<sub>2</sub> adsorption and desorption isotherms were measured by a Quantachrome autosorb iQ. The specific surface area was calculated using the Brunauere-Emmette-Teller (BET) model, and the pore size distribution was determined using the Nonlocal Density Functional Theory (NLDFT). X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) was carried out on a Thermo Scientific spectrometer (ESCALAB 250Xi) equipped with a pass energy of 30&nbsp;eV with a power of 100&nbsp;W (10&nbsp;kV and 10&nbsp;mA), and a monochromatized AlKα X-ray source (h<i>ν</i> = 1486.65&nbsp;eV). The samples for the depth-profiling XPS analysis were disassembled in the glove box after 3 cycles at the fully charged state. The X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) was performed at the soft X-ray synchrotron radiation facility of the Hefei Light Source (HLS). Online thermogravimetry-Fourier transform infrared reflection (TG-FTIR) was collected by a TG analyzer (NETZSCH, STA 449 F3 Jupiter) coupled with an FTIR spectrometer (Thermo Scientific, Nicolet iS50).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0120-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <p>To prepare the work electrode, a slurry of 70 wt% the as-synthesized sample, 20 wt% Super P, and 10 wt% PVDF was coated onto a copper foil. The resulting electrode was dried in a vacuum oven at 60&nbsp;°C overnight and then punched into 12&nbsp;mm circles. The mass loading of the active material was 0.8–1.2&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. All the electrochemical tests were conducted using CR2032-type coin cells. The half cells were prepared by stacking the work electrode, a Whatman glass fiber soaked in 1&nbsp;mol L<sup>−1</sup> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub>/DEGDME, a sodium sheet as the counter electrode, and a gasket in the glove box with H<sub>2</sub>O and O<sub>2</sub> under 0.01 ppm. Galvanostatic charge-discharge and cyclic voltammetry (CV) tests were performed using LAND CT2001 and CHI760, respectively. For the full cell test, the as-prepared anode and the O3-type NaNi<sub>1/3</sub>Fe<sub>1/3</sub>Mn<sub>1/3</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM) cathode were performed in 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> EC/PC (1:1) + 5% FEC.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0130-title"> In Situ Raman Spectroscopy Analysis</h4>
                  
                  <p>For in situ Raman spectroscopy, a free-standing work electrode was obtained using a mixture of p-CNTs@HC-1000, Super P, and PTFE as a binder instead of PVDF, in a weight ratio of 7:2:1. Other conditions for assembling the in situ cell are consistent with the coin cell. The battery was continuously charged/discharged at a current density of 60&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> during the collection process of Raman spectra through a quartz window, which was recorded every 10&nbsp;min.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0140-title"> In Situ XRD Analysis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The work electrode containing ≈10&nbsp;mg of active materials was used to enhance the signal of in situ XRD. The in situ cell was installed in the same process as the coin cell, instead of a Be window on the top. The discharge/charge process was conducted at 60&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> over a voltage range of 0.01 to 1.5&nbsp;V. The XRD patterns were collected every 10&nbsp;min during the GCD test.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0150-title"> In Situ EIS Analysis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The in situ EIS was measured using a coin cell configuration. The discharge/charge process was conducted at 30&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> during the first cycle over a voltage range of 0.01 to 3.0&nbsp;V. The EIS curves were performed over a frequency range from 10<sup>5</sup> to 0.01&nbsp;Hz.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202403144-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202403144-sec-0160-title"> Theoretical Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) was used to implement Density Functional Theory (DFT) calculations. The Perdew-Bruke-Ernzerhof (PBE) functional and generalized gradient approximation (GGA) was used to elucidate the exchange-correlation function. K-points on the Brillouin zone were 2&nbsp;×&nbsp;2&nbsp;×&nbsp;1 with a cut-off energy of 500&nbsp;eV. The convergence criteria for all geometric structures were set to be 0.02&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. The adsorption energy of Na<sup>+</sup> (<i>E</i><sub>ads</sub>) was calculated using the following Equation:

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202403144-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/7f33a32b-4643-4637-8056-63e6dfc41385/adfm202403144-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202403144:adfm202403144-math-0001" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202403144-math-0001.png">
                              <semantics>

                                       </mrow>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mo linebreak="badbreak">=</mo>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>E</mi>
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mi>N</mi>

                                          <mi>u</mi>

                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">−</mo>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mrow>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>N</mi>
                                                <mi>a</mi>
                                             </mrow>
                                          </msub>
                                          <mo>+</mo>
                                          <msub>
                                             <mi>E</mi>
                                             <mrow>
                                                <mi>s</mi>

                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
